<p>The <b>Western Jackdaw</b> (<i>Corvus monedula</i>), sometimes known as the <b>Eurasian Jackdaw</b>, <b>European Jackdaw</b> or simply <b>Jackdaw</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerine</a> bird in the <a href="/wiki/Corvidae" title="Corvidae">crow family</a>. Found across Europe, western Asia and North Africa, it is mostly <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">resident</a>, although northern and eastern populations <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrate</a> south in winter. Four <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> are recognised, which mainly differ in the colouration of the <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> on the head and nape. <a href="/wiki/Carolus_Linnaeus" title="Carolus Linnaeus" class="mw-redirect">Linnaeus</a> first <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">described</a> it formally, giving it the name <i>Corvus monedula</i>. Later <a href="/wiki/Genetic_testing" title="Genetic testing">analysis of its DNA</a> shows that, with its closest relative the <a href="/wiki/Daurian_Jackdaw" title="Daurian Jackdaw">Daurian Jackdaw</a>, it is an early offshoot from the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Corvus_(genus)" title="Corvus (genus)" class="mw-redirect">Corvus</a></i>, and possibly distinct enough to warrant reclassification in a separate genus, <i><a href="/wiki/Coloeus" title="Coloeus">Coloeus</a></i>. The common name derives from the word "jack", meaning "small", and "daw", the native English name for the bird.</p>
<p>Measuring 34–39 centimetres (13–15 in) in length, the Western Jackdaw is a black-plumaged bird with a grey <a href="/wiki/Nape" title="Nape">nape</a> and distinctive pale-grey <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">irises</a>. It is <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gregarious" class="extiw" title="wikt:gregarious">gregarious</a> and vocal, living in small groups with a complex social structure in farmland, open woodland, on coastal cliffs, and in urban settings. An <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivorous</a> and opportunistic feeder, it eats a wide variety of plant material and <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, as well as food waste from urban areas. Western Jackdaws are <a href="/wiki/Monogamous_pairing_in_animals" title="Monogamous pairing in animals">monogamous</a> and build simple nests of sticks in cavities in trees, cliffs, or buildings. About five pale blue or blue-green eggs with brown speckles are laid and incubated by the female. The young then fledge in four to five weeks.</p>

<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Systematics">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Systematics">Systematics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h3>
<p>The Western Jackdaw was one of the many species originally described by <a href="/wiki/Carolus_Linnaeus" title="Carolus Linnaeus" class="mw-redirect">Linnaeus</a> in his 18th century work <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> Owing to its supposed fondness for picking up coins, Linnaeus gave it the binomial name <i>Corvus monedula</i>, choosing the <a href="/wiki/Specific_name_(zoology)" title="Specific name (zoology)">specific name</a> <i>mǒnēdŭla</i>, which is derived from <i><a href="/wiki/Moneta" title="Moneta">moneta</a></i>, the Latin stem of the word "money".<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Coloeus" title="Coloeus">Coloeus</a></i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> κολοιός (<i>koloios</i>) for jackdaw,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> was created by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pallas" title="Peter Pallas" class="mw-redirect">Peter Pallas</a> in 1766, though most subsequent works have retained the two jackdaw species in <i>Corvus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The original Old English word <i>cēo</i> (pronounced with initial <i>ch</i>) gave modern English "<a href="/wiki/Chough" title="Chough">chough</a>"; <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Chaucer</a> sometimes used this word to refer to the Western Jackdaw,<sup id="cite_ref-Good78_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good78-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> as did <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">Shakespeare</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i> although there has been debate about which species he was referring to.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Onomatopoeia" title="Onomatopoeia">onomatopoeic</a> name, based on the Western Jackdaw's call, now refers to corvids of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Pyrrhocorax" title="Pyrrhocorax" class="mw-redirect">Pyrrhocorax</a></i>; the <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_Chough" title="Red-billed Chough">Red-billed Chough</a> (<i>Pyrrhocorax pyrrhocorax</i>), formerly particularly common in Cornwall, became known initially as the "Cornish Chough" and then just the "Chough", the name transferring from one species to the other.<sup id="cite_ref-Cocker_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cocker-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The common name <i>jackdaw</i> first appeared in the 16th century, and is thought to be a compound of the forename <i>Jack</i>, used in animal names to signify a small form (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Jack_Snipe" title="Jack Snipe">Jack Snipe</a>), and the native English word <i>daw</i>. Formerly, Western Jackdaws were simply called "daws".<sup id="cite_ref-Good78_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good78-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> The metallic <i>chyak</i> call may be the origin of the <i>jack</i> part of the common name,<sup id="cite_ref-British_Garden_Birds:_Jackdaw_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_Garden_Birds:_Jackdaw-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> but this is not supported by the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Daw</i>, first used for the bird in the 15th century, is held by the <i>Oxford English Dictionary</i> to be derived from the postulated <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>dawe</i>, citing the <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> in <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <i>tāha</i>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_High_German" title="Middle High German">Middle High German</a> <i>tāhe</i> or <i>tāchele</i>, and modern German <i>Dahle</i> or <i>Dohle</i>, and dialectal <i>Tach, Dähi, Däche</i> and <i>Dacha</i>.</p>
<p>Names in English dialects are numerous. Scottish and north English dialects have included <i>ka</i> or <i>kae</i> since the 14th century. The <a href="/wiki/Midlands_(England)" title="Midlands (England)">Midlands</a> form of this word was <i>co</i> or <i>coo</i>. <i>Caddow</i> is potentially a compound of <i>ka</i> and <i>dow</i>, a variant of <i>daw</i>. Other dialectal or obsolete names include <i>caddesse</i>, <i>cawdaw</i>, <i>caddy</i>, <i>chauk</i>, <i>college-bird</i>, <i>jackerdaw</i>, <i>jacko</i>, <i>ka-wattie</i>, <i>chimney-sweep bird</i> (from their nesting propensities), and <i>sea-crow</i> (from the frequency with which they are found on coasts). It was also frequently known quasi-nominally as <i>Jack</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Svensson_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svensson-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Swainson.2C_Charles_1885_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swainson.2C_Charles_1885-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An archaic <a href="/wiki/Collective_noun" title="Collective noun">collective noun</a> for a group of jackdaws is a "clattering".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> Another name for a flock is a "train".<sup id="cite_ref-white_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span></h3>
<p>A study in 2000 found that the genetic distance between jackdaws and the other members of <i>Corvus</i> was greater than that within the rest of the genus.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> This led <a href="/wiki/Pamela_C._Rasmussen" title="Pamela C. Rasmussen">Pamela Rasmussen</a> to reinstate the genus name <i>Coloeus</i> in her <i><a href="/wiki/Birds_of_South_Asia" title="Birds of South Asia" class="mw-redirect">Birds of South Asia</a></i> (2005),<sup id="cite_ref-ripleyguide_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ripleyguide-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> a treatment also used in a 1982 systematic list in German by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Edmund_Wolters" title="Hans Edmund Wolters">Hans Edmund Wolters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> A study of corvid <a href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect">phylogeny</a> undertaken in 2007 compared DNA sequences in the <a href="/wiki/MtDNA_control_region" title="MtDNA control region">mitochondrial control region</a> of several corvids. It found that the Western Jackdaw, and the closely related <a href="/wiki/Daurian_Jackdaw" title="Daurian Jackdaw">Daurian Jackdaw</a> (<i>C.&#160;dauuricus</i>) of eastern Russia and China, were <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> to the core <i>Corvus</i> <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-haring_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haring-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> The names <i>Coloeus&#160;monedula</i> and <i>Coloeus&#160;dauuricus</i> have since been adopted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Ornithological_Congress" title="International Ornithological Congress">International Ornithological Congress</a> in their official list.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> The two species of jackdaw have been reported to <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridise</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a>, southern Siberia, and Mongolia. Analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> of specimens of the two species from their core ranges show them to be genetically distinct.<sup id="cite_ref-haring_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haring-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Subspecies">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Subspecies">Subspecies</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloeus_monedula_-Munkedal,_Vastra_Gotaland,_Sweden-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Coloeus_monedula_-Munkedal%2C_Vastra_Gotaland%2C_Sweden-8.jpg/220px-Coloeus_monedula_-Munkedal%2C_Vastra_Gotaland%2C_Sweden-8.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Coloeus_monedula_-Munkedal%2C_Vastra_Gotaland%2C_Sweden-8.jpg/330px-Coloeus_monedula_-Munkedal%2C_Vastra_Gotaland%2C_Sweden-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Coloeus_monedula_-Munkedal%2C_Vastra_Gotaland%2C_Sweden-8.jpg/440px-Coloeus_monedula_-Munkedal%2C_Vastra_Gotaland%2C_Sweden-8.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloeus_monedula_-Munkedal,_Vastra_Gotaland,_Sweden-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<i>C. m. monedula</i> in Sweden. This subspecies has a whitish partial collar</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CROP._Corvus_monedula,_naakka,_Naantalin_hautausmaa,_Naantali,_8.11.2011..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CROP._Corvus_monedula%2C_naakka%2C_Naantalin_hautausmaa%2C_Naantali%2C_8.11.2011..jpg/220px-CROP._Corvus_monedula%2C_naakka%2C_Naantalin_hautausmaa%2C_Naantali%2C_8.11.2011..jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CROP._Corvus_monedula%2C_naakka%2C_Naantalin_hautausmaa%2C_Naantali%2C_8.11.2011..jpg/330px-CROP._Corvus_monedula%2C_naakka%2C_Naantalin_hautausmaa%2C_Naantali%2C_8.11.2011..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/CROP._Corvus_monedula%2C_naakka%2C_Naantalin_hautausmaa%2C_Naantali%2C_8.11.2011..jpg/440px-CROP._Corvus_monedula%2C_naakka%2C_Naantalin_hautausmaa%2C_Naantali%2C_8.11.2011..jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CROP._Corvus_monedula,_naakka,_Naantalin_hautausmaa,_Naantali,_8.11.2011..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Leucism" title="Leucism">Leucistic</a> Jackdaw (<i>Corvus monedula</i>) with strange white feathers in Naantali cemetery, <a href="/wiki/Naantali" title="Naantali">Naantali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are four recognised subspecies of the Western Jackdaw.<sup id="cite_ref-Svensson_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svensson-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> All European subspecies <a href="/wiki/Intergrade" title="Intergrade" class="mw-redirect">intergrade</a> where their populations meet.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>C. m. monedula</i> intergrades into <i>C. m. soemmerringii</i> in a transition zone running from Finland south across the Baltic and eastern Poland to Romania and Croatia.<sup id="cite_ref-xs4all.nl_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xs4all.nl-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><i>C. m. monedula</i> (Linnaeus, 1758), the <a href="/wiki/Nominate_subspecies" title="Nominate subspecies" class="mw-redirect">nominate subspecies</a>, is found in eastern Europe. Its range extends across Scandinavia, from southern Finland south to <a href="/wiki/Esbjerg" title="Esbjerg">Esbjerg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Haderslev" title="Haderslev">Haderslev</a> in Denmark, through eastern Germany and Poland, and south across eastern central Europe to the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a> and north-western Romania, <a href="/wiki/Vojvodina" title="Vojvodina">Vojvodina</a> in northern Serbia, and Slovenia.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp120_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp120-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> It breeds in south-eastern Norway, southern Sweden, and northern and eastern Denmark, with occasional wintering in England and France. It has been recorded as a rare <a href="/wiki/Vagrancy_(biology)" title="Vagrancy (biology)">vagrant</a> to Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> It has a pale <a href="/wiki/Nape" title="Nape">nape</a> and sides of the neck, a dark throat, and a light grey partial collar of variable extent.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><i>C. m. spermologus</i> (<a href="/wiki/Louis_Jean_Pierre_Vieillot" title="Louis Jean Pierre Vieillot">Vieillot</a>, 1817) occurs in western and central Europe from the British Isles, Netherlands and the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a> in the north, through western Switzerland into Italy in the south-east, and the Iberian peninsula and Morocco in the south.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp120_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp120-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> It winters in the Canary Islands and Corsica. The name <i>"spermologus"</i> comes from the Greek σπερμολόγος, <i>a picker up of seeds</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> It is darker in colour than the other subspecies and lacks the whitish border at the base of the grey nape.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><i>C. m. soemmerringii</i> (<a href="/wiki/Johann_Fischer_von_Waldheim" title="Johann Fischer von Waldheim" class="mw-redirect">Fischer</a>, 1811) is found in north-eastern Europe and north and central Asia, from the former Soviet Union to <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> and north-west Mongolia, and south to Turkey, Israel and the eastern Himalayas. Its south-western limits are Serbia and southern Romania.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp120_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp120-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> It winters in Iran and northern India (<a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-ripleyguide_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ripleyguide-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johann_Fischer_von_Waldheim" title="Johann Fischer von Waldheim" class="mw-redirect">Johann Fischer von Waldheim</a> described this <a href="/wiki/Taxon" title="Taxon">taxon</a> as <i>Corvus soemmerringii</i> in 1811, noting its differences from populations in western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-fischer1811_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fischer1811-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> Its species name was given in honour of the German anatomist, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Thomas_von_S%C3%B6mmerring" title="Samuel Thomas von Sömmerring">Samuel Thomas von Sömmerring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> It is distinguished by the nape and the sides of the neck being paler, creating a contrasting black crown and lighter grey part collar.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><i>C. m. cirtensis</i> (<a href="/wiki/Walter_Rothschild,_2nd_Baron_Rothschild" title="Walter Rothschild, 2nd Baron Rothschild">Rothschild</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Hartert" title="Ernst Hartert">Hartert</a>, 1912) is found in Morocco and Algeria in north-western Africa and was formerly found in Tunisia.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp120_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp120-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> The name <i>"cirtensis"</i> refers to the ancient city of <a href="/wiki/Cirta" title="Cirta">Cirta</a> in <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> is duller and more uniformly dark grey than the other subspecies, with the paler nape less distinct.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jackdaw_-_up_close_and_personal_(552502080).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Jackdaw_-_up_close_and_personal_%28552502080%29.jpg/220px-Jackdaw_-_up_close_and_personal_%28552502080%29.jpg" width="220" height="198" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Jackdaw_-_up_close_and_personal_%28552502080%29.jpg/330px-Jackdaw_-_up_close_and_personal_%28552502080%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Jackdaw_-_up_close_and_personal_%28552502080%29.jpg/440px-Jackdaw_-_up_close_and_personal_%28552502080%29.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jackdaw_-_up_close_and_personal_(552502080).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Adult of subspecies <i>spermologus</i>, Dornoch, Scotland. The <a href="/wiki/Beak#Rictal_bristles" title="Beak">rictal bristles</a> cover much of the bill.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloeus_monedula_-Newcastle-Upon-Tyne,_England_-juvenile-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Coloeus_monedula_-Newcastle-Upon-Tyne%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg/220px-Coloeus_monedula_-Newcastle-Upon-Tyne%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Coloeus_monedula_-Newcastle-Upon-Tyne%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg/330px-Coloeus_monedula_-Newcastle-Upon-Tyne%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Coloeus_monedula_-Newcastle-Upon-Tyne%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg/440px-Coloeus_monedula_-Newcastle-Upon-Tyne%2C_England_-juvenile-8.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloeus_monedula_-Newcastle-Upon-Tyne,_England_-juvenile-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Juvenile of subspecies <i>spermologus</i>, Newcastle-upon-Tyne, England</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Measuring 34–39 centimetres (13–15 in) in length, the Western Jackdaw is the second smallest member of the genus <i>Corvus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodwin74_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodwin74-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Most of the plumage is a shiny black, with a purple (in subspecies <i>monedula</i> and <i>spermologus</i>) or blue (in subspecies <i>cirtensis</i> and <i>soemmerringii</i>) sheen on the crown,<sup id="cite_ref-cramp139_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp139-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> forehead, and <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Secondaries" title="Flight feather">secondaries</a>, and a green-blue sheen on the throat, <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Primaries" title="Flight feather">primaries</a>, and tail. The cheeks, nape and neck are light grey to greyish-silver, and the underparts are slate-grey. The legs are black, as is the short stout bill,<sup id="cite_ref-Goodwin74_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodwin74-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> the length of which is about 75% of the length of the rest of the head.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp139_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp139-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> There are <a href="/wiki/Beak#Rictal_bristles" title="Beak">rictal bristles</a> covering around 40% of the upper mandible and 25% of the lower mandible.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp139_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp139-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">irises</a> of adults are greyish or silvery white while those of juveniles are light blue, becoming brownish before whitening at around one year of age.<sup id="cite_ref-Goodwin74_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goodwin74-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The sexes look alike,<sup id="cite_ref-Svensson_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svensson-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> though the head and neck plumage of male birds fades more with age and wear, particularly just before <a href="/wiki/Moulting#Birds" title="Moulting">moulting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp137_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp137-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> Western Jackdaws undergo a complete moult from June to September in the western parts of their range, and a month later in the east.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp120_25-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp120-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> The purplish sheen of the cap is most prominent just after moulting.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp137_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp137-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Immature birds have duller and less demarcated plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp121_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp121-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> The head is a sooty black, sometimes with a faint greenish sheen and brown feather bases visible; the back and side of the neck are dark grey and the underparts greyish or sooty black. The tail has narrower feathers and a greenish sheen.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp137_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp137-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There is very little geographic variation in size. The main differences are the presence or absence of a whitish partial collar at the base of the nape, the variations in the shade of the nape and the tone of the underparts. Populations in central Asia have slightly larger wings and western populations have a slightly heavier bill. Body colour becomes darker further north, in mountain regions and humid climates, and paler elsewhere. However, individual variation, particularly in juveniles and also during the months before moulting, can often be greater than geographic differences.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp139_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp139-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A skilled flyer, the Western Jackdaw can manoeuvre tightly as well as tumble and glide. It has characteristic jerky wing beats when flying, though these are not evident when birds are <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrating</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp121_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp121-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wind_tunnel" title="Wind tunnel">Wind tunnel</a> experiments show that the preferred gliding speed is between 6 and 11 metres (20 and 36 ft) per second and that the wingspan decreases as the bird flies faster.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> On the ground, Western Jackdaws have an upright posture and strut briskly, their short legs giving them a rapid gait. They feed with their heads held down or horizontally.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp121_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp121-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Within its range, the Western Jackdaw is unmistakable; its short bill and grey nape are distinguishing features. From a distance, it can be confused with a <a href="/wiki/Rook_(bird)" title="Rook (bird)">Rook</a> (<i>Corvus frugilegus</i>), or when in flight, with a <a href="/wiki/Pigeon" title="Pigeon" class="mw-redirect">pigeon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chough" title="Chough">chough</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp121_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp121-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Flying Western Jackdaws are distinguishable from other corvids by their smaller size, faster and deeper wingbeats and proportionately narrower and less fingered wing tips. They also have shorter, thicker necks, much shorter bills and frequently fly in tighter flocks. They can be distinguished from choughs by their uniformly grey underwings and their black beaks and legs.<sup id="cite_ref-BWP_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BWP-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The Western Jackdaw is very similar in morphology, behaviour, and calls to the Daurian Jackdaw, with which its range overlaps in western Asia. Adults are readily distinguished, since the Daurian has a pied plumage, but immature birds are much more similar, both species having dark plumage and dark eyes. The Daurian tends to be darker, with a less contrasting nape than the Western.<sup id="cite_ref-madge_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madge-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Voice">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Voice">Voice</span></h3>
<p>Western Jackdaws are voluble birds. The main call, frequently given in flight, is a metallic and squeaky <i>chyak-chyak</i> or <i>kak-kak</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Svensson_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svensson-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> This is a contact or greeting call. A feeding call made by adults to call young, or males when offering food to their mates, has been transcribed as <i>kiaw</i> or <i>kyow</i>. Females in return give a more drawn out version when begging for food from males, written as <i>kyaay</i>, <i>tchaayk</i> or <i>giaaaa</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp133_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp133-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Perched birds often chatter together, and before settling for the night, large roosting flocks make a cackling noise. Western Jackdaws also have a hoarse, drawn-out <a href="/wiki/Alarm_call" title="Alarm call" class="mw-redirect">alarm call</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Svensson_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svensson-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>arrrrr</i> or <i>kaaaarr</i>, used when warning of predators or when mobbing them.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp133_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp133-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Nestlings begin making a soft <i>cheep</i> at about a week of age. As they grow, their voice becomes louder until their call is a penetrating screech around day 18. After this, the voice deepens and softens.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp134_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp134-39"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> From day 25, the young cease calling and become silent if they hear an unfamiliar noise.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp132_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp132-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span></h2>
<p>The Western Jackdaw is found from north-west Africa through all of Europe, except for the extreme north, and eastwards through <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">central Asia</a> to the eastern <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and Lake Baikal. To the east, it occurs throughout Turkey, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, Iran, Iraq, Afghanistan, Pakistan, and north-west India.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> However, it is regionally extinct in Malta and Tunisia.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_0-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-0"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> The range is vast, with an estimated global extent of between 1 million and 10 million square kilometres (4 hundred thousand to 4 million square miles). It has a large global population, with an estimated 15.6 to 45 million individuals in Europe alone.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Censuses of bird populations in marginal uplands in Britain show that Western Jackdaws greatly increased in numbers between the 1970s and 2010, although this increase may be related to recovery from previous periods when they were regarded as pests.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> The UK population was estimated at 2.5 million individuals in 1998, up from 780,000 in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Most populations are <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/resident" class="extiw" title="wikt:resident">resident</a>, but the northern and eastern populations are more migratory,<sup id="cite_ref-xs4all.nl_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xs4all.nl-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> relocating to wintering areas between September and November and returning between February and early May.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp124_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp124-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> Their range expands northwards into Russia to Siberia during summer, and retracts in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Svensson_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svensson-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> They are vagrants to the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, particularly in the winter and spring, and occasionally to <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp121_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp121-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Elsewhere, Western Jackdaws congregate over winter in the <a href="/wiki/Ural_River" title="Ural River">Ural Valley</a> in north-western Kazakhstan, the north <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Depression" title="Caspian Depression">Caspian</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> region of western China. They are winter visitors to the <a href="/wiki/Quetta" title="Quetta">Quetta Valley</a> in western Pakistan,<sup id="cite_ref-cramp124_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp124-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup> and are winter vagrants to Lebanon, where they were first recorded in 1962.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> In Syria, they are winter vagrants and rare residents with some confirmed breeding taking place.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The <i>soemmerringii</i> subspecies occurs in south-central Siberia and extreme north-west China and is accidental to <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> A small number of Western Jackdaws reached the north-east of North America in the 1980s and have been found from Atlantic Canada to Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> They have also occurred as vagrants in <a href="/wiki/Gibraltar" title="Gibraltar">Gibraltar</a>, Mauritania, and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Pierre_and_Miquelon" title="Saint Pierre and Miquelon">Saint Pierre and Miquelon</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_0-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-0"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> and one is reported to have been seen in Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Western Jackdaws inhabit wooded <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a>, pastures, cultivated land, coastal cliffs, and towns. They thrive when forested areas are cleared and converted to fields and open areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Habitats with a mix of large trees, buildings, and open ground are preferred; open fields are left to the Rook, and more wooded areas to the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Jay" title="Eurasian Jay">Eurasian Jay</a> (<i>Garrulus glandarius</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-cramp121_34-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp121-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Along with other corvids such as the Rook, <a href="/wiki/Common_Raven" title="Common Raven">Common Raven</a> (<i>Corvus corax</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Hooded_Crow" title="Hooded Crow">Hooded Crow</a> (<i>C. cornix</i>), some Western Jackdaws spend the winter in urban parks; populations measured in three urban parks in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a> show increases from October to December, possibly due to Western Jackdaws migrating there from areas further north.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> The same data from Warsaw, collected from 1977 to 2003, showed that the wintering Western Jackdaw population had increased four-fold. The cause of the increase is unknown, but a reduction in the number of Rooks may have benefited the species locally, or Rooks overwintering in Belarus may have caused Western Jackdaws to relocate to Warsaw.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloeus_monedula_-Bushy_Park,_London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames,_England_-several-8_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coloeus_monedula_-Bushy_Park%2C_London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames%2C_England_-several-8_%281%29.jpg/220px-Coloeus_monedula_-Bushy_Park%2C_London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames%2C_England_-several-8_%281%29.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coloeus_monedula_-Bushy_Park%2C_London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames%2C_England_-several-8_%281%29.jpg/330px-Coloeus_monedula_-Bushy_Park%2C_London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames%2C_England_-several-8_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coloeus_monedula_-Bushy_Park%2C_London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames%2C_England_-several-8_%281%29.jpg/440px-Coloeus_monedula_-Bushy_Park%2C_London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames%2C_England_-several-8_%281%29.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloeus_monedula_-Bushy_Park,_London_Borough_of_Richmond_upon_Thames,_England_-several-8_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A family group in <a href="/wiki/Bushy_Park" title="Bushy Park">Bushy Park</a>, London</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Generally wary of people in the forest or countryside, Western Jackdaws are much tamer in urban areas.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp129_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp129-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Like <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Magpie" title="Eurasian Magpie">Magpies</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> they are known to pick up shiny objects such as <a href="/wiki/Jewellery" title="Jewellery">jewellery</a> to hoard in nests.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Gay" title="John Gay">John Gay</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Beggar%27s_Opera" title="Beggar's Opera" class="mw-redirect">Beggar's Opera</a></i>, notes that "A covetous fellow, like a jackdaw, steals what he was never made to enjoy, for the sake of hiding it".<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Smollett" title="Tobias Smollett">Tobias Smollett</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Expedition_of_Humphry_Clinker" title="The Expedition of Humphry Clinker">The Expedition of Humphry Clinker</a></i>, a scathing character assassination runs, "He is ungracious as a hog, greedy as a vulture, and thievish as a jackdaw."<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Highly <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/gregarious" class="extiw" title="wikt:gregarious">gregarious</a>, Western Jackdaws are generally seen in flocks of varying sizes, though males and females pair-bond for life and pairs stay together within flocks.<sup id="cite_ref-Henderson_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> Flocks increase in size in autumn and birds congregate at dusk for communal roosting,<sup id="cite_ref-Svensson_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Svensson-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> with up to several thousand individuals gathering at one site. At <a href="/wiki/Uppsala" title="Uppsala">Uppsala</a>, Sweden, 40,000 birds have been recorded at a single winter roost with mated pairs often settling together for the night.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp129_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp129-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Western Jackdaws frequently congregate with Hooded Crows<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> or Rooks,<sup id="cite_ref-cramp121_34-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp121-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> the latter particularly when migrating or roosting.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp128_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp128-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> They have been recorded foraging with the <a href="/wiki/Common_Starling" title="Common Starling">Common Starling</a> (<i>Sturnus vulgaris</i>), <a href="/wiki/Northern_Lapwing" title="Northern Lapwing">Northern Lapwing</a> (<i>Vanellus vanellus</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Common_Gull" title="Common Gull">Common Gull</a> (<i>Larus canus</i>) in northwestern England.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp128_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp128-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Flocks are targets of coordinated hunting by pairs of <a href="/wiki/Lanner_Falcon" title="Lanner Falcon">Lanner Falcons</a> (<i>Falco biarmicus</i>), although larger groups are more able to elude the <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predators</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Western Jackdaws sometimes mob and drive off larger birds such as European Magpies, Common Ravens, or <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Vulture" title="Egyptian Vulture">Egyptian Vultures</a> (<i>Neophron percnopterus</i>); one gives an alarm call which alerts its conspecifics to gather and attack as a group.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp132_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp132-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Occasionally, a sick or injured Western Jackdaw is mobbed until it is killed.<sup id="cite_ref-wilmore_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilmore-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In his book <i><a href="/wiki/King_Solomon%27s_Ring_(nonfiction)" title="King Solomon's Ring (nonfiction)">King Solomon's Ring</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Lorenz" title="Konrad Lorenz">Konrad Lorenz</a> described and analysed the complex social interactions in a Western Jackdaw flock that lived around his house in <a href="/wiki/Sankt_Andr%C3%A4-W%C3%B6rdern" title="Sankt Andrä-Wördern">Altenberg</a>, Austria. He ringed them for identification and caged them in the winter to prevent their annual <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migration</a>. He found that the birds have a <a href="/wiki/Dominance_hierarchy" title="Dominance hierarchy">linear hierarchical group structure</a>, with higher-ranked individuals dominating lower-ranked birds, and pair-bonded birds sharing the same rank.<sup id="cite_ref-Lorenz_1964_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorenz_1964-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Young males establish their individual status before pairing with females. Upon pairing, the female assumes the same social position as her partner. Unmated females are the lowest members in the pecking order, and are the last to have access to food and shelter.<sup id="cite_ref-Lorenz_1964_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorenz_1964-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Lorenz noted one case in which a male, absent during the dominance struggles and pair bondings, returned to the flock, became the dominant male, and chose one of two unpaired females for a mate. This female immediately assumed a dominant position in the social hierarchy and demonstrated this by pecking others. According to Lorenz, the most significant factor in social behaviour was the immediate and intuitive grasp of the new hierarchy by each of the Western Jackdaws in the flock.<sup id="cite_ref-Lorenz_1964_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lorenz_1964-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Social displays">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Social_displays">Social displays</span></h3>
<p>Social hierarchy in Western Jackdaw flocks is determined by supplanting, fighting, and <a href="/wiki/Threat_display" title="Threat display">threat displays</a>—several of which have been described. In the <i>bill-up posture</i>, the Western Jackdaw tilts its bill and head upwards and sleeks its plumage. Indicating both appeasement and assertiveness, the posture is used by birds intending to enter feeding flocks. A <i>bill-down posture</i> is another commonly used <a href="/wiki/Agonistic_behaviour" title="Agonistic behaviour">agonistic behaviour</a>. In this display, a bird lowers its bill and erects its nape and head feathers, and sometimes slightly lifts its wings. Western Jackdaws often face off in this posture until one backs down or a fight ensues. In the <i>forward-threat posture</i>, a bird holds its body horizontally and thrusts its head forwards. In intense versions, the bird ruffles its feathers and spreads or raises its tail and wings. This extreme is seen when facing off over nests or females.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp130_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp130-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> In the <i>defensive-threat posture</i>, the bird lowers its head and bill, spreads its tail and ruffles its feathers. <i>Supplanting</i> is where one bird moves in and displaces another from a perch-site. The second bird usually retreats without resorting to a fight. Western Jackdaws fight by launching themselves at each other feet-first and then wrestling with their feet intertwined and pecking at each other. Other individuals gather and call noisily.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp131_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp131-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Western Jackdaws entreat their partners to preen them by showing their nape and ruffling their head feathers. Birds mainly preen each other's head and neck. Known as <i>allopreening</i>, this behaviour is almost always done between birds of a mated pair.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp131_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp131-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_monedula_009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Corvus_monedula_009.jpg/220px-Corvus_monedula_009.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Corvus_monedula_009.jpg/330px-Corvus_monedula_009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Corvus_monedula_009.jpg/440px-Corvus_monedula_009.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_monedula_009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Subspecies <i>soemmerringii</i>, foraging in pasture in Russia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Foraging takes place mostly on the ground in open areas and to some extent in trees.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">Rubbish tips</a>, bins, streets, and gardens are also visited, more often early in the morning when there are fewer people about.<sup id="cite_ref-Good75_23-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Good75-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Various feeding methods are employed, such as jumping, pecking, clod-turning and scattering, probing the soil, and occasionally, digging. <a href="/wiki/Fly" title="Fly">Flies</a> around <a href="/wiki/Cow_dung" title="Cow dung">cow pats</a> are caught by jumping from the ground or at times by dropping vertically from a few metres onto the cow pat. <a href="/wiki/Earthworm" title="Earthworm">Earthworms</a> are not usually extracted from the ground by Western Jackdaws but are eaten from freshly ploughed soil.<sup id="cite_ref-lockie_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lockie-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Jackdaws will ride on the backs of sheep and other mammals, seeking <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a> as well as actively gathering wool or hair for nests, and will catch flying ants in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-madge_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madge-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> Compared with other corvids, the Western Jackdaw spends more time exploring and turning over objects with its bill; it also has a straighter and less downturned bill and increased binocular vision which are advantageous for this foraging strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Western Jackdaw tends to feed on small <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> up to 18 millimetres (0.71&#160;in) in length that are found above ground, including various species of <a href="/wiki/Coleoptera" title="Coleoptera" class="mw-redirect">beetle</a> (particularly cockchafers of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Melolontha" title="Melolontha">Melolontha</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-cramp125_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp125-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Curculionidae" title="Curculionidae">weevil</a> <a href="/wiki/Larva" title="Larva">larvae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pupa" title="Pupa">pupae</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lockie_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lockie-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Diptera" title="Diptera" class="mw-redirect">Diptera</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lepidoptera" title="Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a> species, as well as snails and spiders. Also eaten are small rodents, the eggs and chicks of birds, and <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Roadkill" title="Roadkill">roadkill</a>. Vegetable items consumed include farm grains (barley, wheat and oats), weed seeds, elderberries, acorns, and various cultivated fruits.<sup id="cite_ref-lockie_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lockie-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Examination of the <a href="/wiki/Gizzard" title="Gizzard">gizzards</a> of Western Jackdaws shot in Cyprus in spring and summer revealed a diet of cereals (predominantly wheat) and insects (notably <a href="/wiki/Cicada" title="Cicada">cicadas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Hadjisterkotis03_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadjisterkotis03-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> The diet averages 84% plant material except when breeding, when the main food source is insects.<sup id="cite_ref-madge_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-madge-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> A study in southern Spain examining Western Jackdaw pellets found that they contained significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_dioxide" title="Silicon dioxide">silicaceous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcareous</a> grit to aid digestion of vegetable food and supply dietary calcium.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Opportunistic and highly adaptable, the Western Jackdaw varies its diet markedly depending on available food sources.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp127_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp127-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> They have been recorded taking eggs and nestlings from the nests of the <a href="/wiki/Skylark" title="Skylark" class="mw-redirect">Skylark</a> (<i>Alauda arvensis</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manx_Shearwater" title="Manx Shearwater">Manx Shearwater</a> (<i>Puffinus puffinus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Razorbill" title="Razorbill">Razorbill</a> (<i>Alca torda</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_Murre" title="Common Murre">Common Murre</a> (<i>Uria aalge</i>), <a href="/wiki/Grey_Heron" title="Grey Heron">Grey Heron</a> (<i>Ardea cinerea</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-cramp125_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp125-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rock_Pigeon" title="Rock Pigeon" class="mw-redirect">Rock Pigeon</a> (<i>Columba livia</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Collared_Dove" title="Eurasian Collared Dove">Eurasian Collared Dove</a> (<i>Streptopelia decaocto</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-cramp125_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp125-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> A field study of a large city dump on the outskirts of <a href="/wiki/Le%C3%B3n,_Spain" title="León, Spain">León</a> in northwestern Spain showed that Western Jackdaws forage there in the early morning and at dusk, and engage in some degree of <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Saker_Falcon" title="Saker Falcon">Saker Falcon</a> (<i>Falco cherrug</i>) has been reported stealing food from Western Jackdaws on powerlines in <a href="/wiki/Vojvodina" title="Vojvodina">Vojvodina</a> in Serbia.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Western Jackdaws practice active food sharing&#160;– where the initiative for the transfer lies with the donor&#160;– with a number of individuals, regardless of sex or kinship. They also share more of a preferred food than a less preferred food.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The active giving of food by most birds is found mainly in the context of parental care and courtship. Western Jackdaws show much higher levels of active giving than has been documented for other species, including <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a>. The function of this behaviour is not fully understood, though it has been found to be detached from nutrition and compatible with hypotheses of <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">mutualism</a>, reciprocity and harassment avoidance. It has also been proposed that food sharing may be motivated by prestige enhancement.<sup id="cite_ref-AMP_v_Bayern.2C_SR_de_Kort.2C_NS_Clayton.2C_NJ_Emery_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMP_v_Bayern.2C_SR_de_Kort.2C_NS_Clayton.2C_NJ_Emery-74"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloeus_monedula_-Conwy_Castle,_Clwyd,_Wales-8_(2).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coloeus_monedula_-Conwy_Castle%2C_Clwyd%2C_Wales-8_%282%29.jpg/220px-Coloeus_monedula_-Conwy_Castle%2C_Clwyd%2C_Wales-8_%282%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coloeus_monedula_-Conwy_Castle%2C_Clwyd%2C_Wales-8_%282%29.jpg/330px-Coloeus_monedula_-Conwy_Castle%2C_Clwyd%2C_Wales-8_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Coloeus_monedula_-Conwy_Castle%2C_Clwyd%2C_Wales-8_%282%29.jpg/440px-Coloeus_monedula_-Conwy_Castle%2C_Clwyd%2C_Wales-8_%282%29.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coloeus_monedula_-Conwy_Castle,_Clwyd,_Wales-8_(2).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Occupying a hole in a wall at <a href="/wiki/Conwy_Castle" title="Conwy Castle">Conwy Castle</a>, Wales</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jackdaw_brood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jackdaw_brood.jpg/220px-Jackdaw_brood.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jackdaw_brood.jpg/330px-Jackdaw_brood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Jackdaw_brood.jpg/440px-Jackdaw_brood.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jackdaw_brood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Nest with a chick and eggs</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_monedula_fledgling_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Corvus_monedula_fledgling_2.jpg/220px-Corvus_monedula_fledgling_2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Corvus_monedula_fledgling_2.jpg/330px-Corvus_monedula_fledgling_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Corvus_monedula_fledgling_2.jpg/440px-Corvus_monedula_fledgling_2.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Corvus_monedula_fledgling_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Fledgling of subspecies <i>spermologus</i> in southern England</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Western Jackdaws become sexually mature in their second year. Genetic analysis of pairs and offspring shows no evidence of <a href="/wiki/Extra-pair_copulation" title="Extra-pair copulation" class="mw-redirect">extra-pair copulation</a> <sup id="cite_ref-Henderson_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henderson-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> and there is little evidence for couple separation even after multiple instances of reproductive failure.<sup id="cite_ref-emery_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emery-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Some pairs do separate in the first few months, but almost all pairings of over six months' duration are lifelong, ending only when a partner dies.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp128_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp128-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Widowed or separated birds fare badly, often being ousted from nests or territories and unable to rear broods alone.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp128_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp128-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Western Jackdaws usually breed in <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">colonies</a> with pairs collaborating to find a nest site, which they then defend from other pairs and predators during most of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-emery_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-emery-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> They nest in cavities in trees or cliffs, in ruined or occupied buildings and in chimneys, the common feature being a sheltered site for the nest. The availability of suitable sites influences their presence in a locale.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp121_34-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp121-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> They may also use church steeples for nesting, a fact reported in verse by 18th century English poet <a href="/wiki/William_Cowper" title="William Cowper">William Cowper</a>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">
<p>A great frequenter of the church,<i><br /></i> Where, bishoplike, he finds a perch,<i><br /></i></p>
And dormitory too.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>A mated pair usually constructs a nest by improving crevice by dropping sticks into it; it is then built on top of the platform formed.<sup id="cite_ref-wilmore_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilmore-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> This behaviour has led to the blocking of chimneys and even resulted in nests crashing down into fireplaces, sometimes with birds still on them.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Nest platforms can attain a great size. John Mason Neale notes that a "Clerk was allowed by the Churchwarden to have for his own use all that the caddows had brought into the Tower: and he took home, at one time, two cart-loads of good firewood, besides a great quantity of rubbish which he threw away."<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Natural_History_and_Antiquities_of_Selborne" title="The Natural History and Antiquities of Selborne">The Natural History of Selborne</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_White" title="Gilbert White">Gilbert White</a> notes that Western Jackdaws used to nest in crevices beneath the <a href="/wiki/Lintel" title="Lintel" class="mw-redirect">lintels</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a>, and describes an example of the bird using a rabbit burrow for nesting.<sup id="cite_ref-white_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-white-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> The species has been recorded outcompeting the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_Owl" title="Tawny Owl">Tawny Owl</a> (<i>Strix aluco</i>) for nest sites in the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> They can take over old nest sites of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Woodpecker" title="Black Woodpecker">Black Woodpecker</a> (<i>Dryocopus martius</i>) <sup id="cite_ref-johnson94_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson94-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Stock_Dove" title="Stock Dove">Stock Dove</a> (<i>Columba oenas</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-cramp131_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp131-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Breeding colonies may also edge out those of the Red-billed Chough, but in turn be ousted by larger corvids such as the Carrion Crow, Rook or Magpie.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp131_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp131-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Nests are lined with hair, wool, dead grass and many other materials.<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a> are a lighter colour than those of other corvids,<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> being smooth, a glossy pale blue or blue-green with darker speckles ranging from dark brown to olive or grey-violet.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp135_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp135-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> Egg size and weight varies slightly between subspecies; those of subspecies <i>monedula</i> average 35.0 by 24.7 millimetres (1.38&#160;in ×&#160;0.97&#160;in) and 11.1&#160;g (0.39&#160;oz) in weight, those of subspecies <i>soemmerringii</i> 34.8 by 25.0 millimetres (1.37&#160;in ×&#160;0.98&#160;in) in size and 11.3&#160;g (0.40&#160;oz) in weight, and those of subspecies <i>spermologus</i> 35.0 by 25.2 millimetres (1.38&#160;in ×&#160;0.99&#160;in) in size and 11.5&#160;g (0.41&#160;oz) in weight.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp135_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp135-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> Clutches usually contain 4 or 5 eggs,<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> although a Slovakian study found clutch sizes ranging from 2 to 9 eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> The eggs are incubated by the female for 17–18 days until hatching as naked <a href="/wiki/Altricial" title="Altricial">altricial</a> chicks, which are completely dependent on the adults for food. They <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a> after 28–35 days,<sup id="cite_ref-Coward_81-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coward-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> and the parents continue to feed them for another four weeks or so.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp128_57-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp128-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Western Jackdaws hatch asynchronously and incubation begins before clutch completion, which often leads to the death of the last-hatched young. If the supply of food is low, parental investment in the brood is kept to a minimum as little energy is wasted on feeding a chick that is unlikely to survive.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Replacement clutches are very rarely laid in the event of clutch failure.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp135_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp135-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Spotted_Cuckoo" title="Great Spotted Cuckoo">Great Spotted Cuckoo</a> (<i>Clamator glandarius</i>) has been recorded as a <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasite</a> of the Western Jackdaw, depositing its eggs in their nests in Spain and Israel.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Nest robbers include the Common Raven in Spain, Tawny Owl, and <a href="/wiki/Least_weasel" title="Least weasel">least weasel</a> (<i>Mustela nivalis</i>) in England, and <a href="/wiki/Brown_rat" title="Brown rat">brown rat</a> (<i>Rattus norvegicus</i>) in Finland.<sup id="cite_ref-cramp136_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cramp136-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_pine_marten" title="European pine marten">European pine marten</a> (<i>Martes martes</i>) raids isolated nests in Sweden but is less successful when nests are part of a colony.<sup id="cite_ref-johnson94_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-johnson94-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Parasites and diseases">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites_and_diseases">Parasites and diseases</span></h3>
<p>Western Jackdaws have learned to peck open the foil caps of milk bottles left on the doorsteps after delivery by the milkman. The bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Campylobacter_jejuni" title="Campylobacter jejuni">Campylobacter jejuni</a></i> has been isolated from their beaks and <a href="/wiki/Cloaca" title="Cloaca">cloacae</a> so milk can become contaminated as they drink. This activity was linked to cases of <i>Campylobacter</i> <a href="/wiki/Gastroenteritis" title="Gastroenteritis">gastroenteritis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gateshead" title="Gateshead">Gateshead</a> in northeast England <sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> and led the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Health_(United_Kingdom)" title="Department of Health (United Kingdom)">Department of Health</a> to suggest that milk from bottles which had been pecked open should be discarded. It was recommended that steps be taken to prevent birds attacking bottles in future.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>An outbreak of a gastrointestinal illness in Spain which was causing mortalities in humans has been linked to Western Jackdaws. During a <a href="/wiki/Autopsy" title="Autopsy">post-mortem</a> on an affected bird, a <a href="/wiki/Polyomaviridae" title="Polyomaviridae">polyomavirus</a> was isolated from the <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a>. The illness appeared to be a co-infection of this with <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> and the virus has been provisionally named the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Crow_polyomavirus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Crow polyomavirus (page does not exist)">crow polyomavirus</a> (CPyV).<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Segmented_filamentous_bacteria" title="Segmented filamentous bacteria">Segmented filamentous bacteria</a> have been isolated from the small intestine of a Western Jackdaw, although their <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogenicity</a> or role is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Interaction with humans">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Interaction_with_humans">Interaction with humans</span></h2>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cultural depictions and folklore">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_depictions_and_folklore">Cultural depictions and folklore</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Borrowed_plumes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Borrowed_plumes.jpg/220px-Borrowed_plumes.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Borrowed_plumes.jpg/330px-Borrowed_plumes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Borrowed_plumes.jpg/440px-Borrowed_plumes.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Borrowed_plumes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.21wmf2/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Harrison_Weir" title="Harrison Weir">Harrison Weir</a>'s 1881 illustration of a vain jackdaw wearing peacock feathers for "<a href="/wiki/The_Bird_in_Borrowed_Feathers" title="The Bird in Borrowed Feathers">The Bird in Borrowed Feathers</a>" (also known as "The Vain Jackdaw") fable<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-fable_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-fable-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In Ancient Greek folklore, a jackdaw can be caught with a dish of oil. A <a href="/wiki/Narcissus_(mythology)" title="Narcissus (mythology)">narcissistic</a> creature, it falls in while looking at its own reflection.<sup id="cite_ref-wentworth_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wentworth-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Aesop%27s_Fables" title="Aesop's Fables">Aesop's Fables</a>, the jackdaw embodies vanity in "<a href="/wiki/The_Bird_in_Borrowed_Feathers" title="The Bird in Borrowed Feathers">The Bird in Borrowed Feathers</a>" (also known as "The Vain Jackdaw"), in which it sought to become king of the birds adorned with feathers of other birds, but was shamed when they fell off.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-fable_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-fable-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> Another fable highlights its stupidity as the jackdaw starves while waiting for figs on a fig tree to ripen.<sup id="cite_ref-deVries76_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> An ancient Greek and Roman adage runs "The swans will sing when the jackdaws are silent", meaning that educated or wise people will speak only after the foolish have become quiet.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> The mythical <a href="/wiki/Arne_(mythology)" title="Arne (mythology)">Princess Arne</a> was bribed with gold by <a href="/wiki/Minos" title="Minos">King Minos</a> of Crete, and was punished for her greed by being transformed into an equally avaricious jackdaw, who still seeks shiny things.<sup id="cite_ref-Graves1589_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graves1589-97"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Roman poet <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a> described jackdaws as harbingers of rain in his poetic work <i><a href="/wiki/Amores_(Ovid)" title="Amores (Ovid)">Amores</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-deVries76_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> notes how the <a href="/wiki/Thessalian" title="Thessalian" class="mw-redirect">Thessalians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrians</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lemnian" title="Lemnian" class="mw-redirect">Lemnians</a> cherished jackdaws for destroying grasshoppers' eggs. The <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Veneti" title="Adriatic Veneti">Veneti</a> are fabled to have bribed the jackdaws to spare their crops.<sup id="cite_ref-wentworth_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wentworth-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In some cultures, a jackdaw on the roof is said to predict a new arrival; alternatively, a jackdaw settling on the roof of a house or flying down a chimney is an omen of death, and coming across one is considered a bad omen.<sup id="cite_ref-deVries76_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deVries76-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> A jackdaw standing on the vanes of a cathedral tower is said to foretell rain. The 12th century historian <a href="/wiki/William_of_Malmesbury" title="William of Malmesbury">William of Malmesbury</a> records the story of a woman who, upon hearing a jackdaw chattering "more loudly than usual," grew pale and became fearful of suffering a "dreadful calamity", and that "while yet speaking, the messenger of her misfortunes arrived".<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Czechs" title="Czechs">Czech</a> superstition formerly held that if jackdaws are seen quarrelling, war will follow, and that jackdaws will not build nests at <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A1zava_(Bene%C5%A1ov_District)" title="Sázava (Benešov District)">Sázava</a> after being banished by <a href="/wiki/Procopius_of_S%C3%A1zava" title="Procopius of Sázava">Saint Procopius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Swainson.2C_Charles_1885_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swainson.2C_Charles_1885-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The jackdaw was considered sacred in Welsh folklore as it nested in church steeples&#160;– it was shunned by the Devil because of its choice of residence.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> 19th century belief in <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">The Fens</a> held that seeing a jackdaw on the way to a wedding was a good omen for a bride.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> People born within the <a href="/wiki/Conwy_town_walls" title="Conwy town walls">town walls</a> of <a href="/wiki/Conwy" title="Conwy">Conwy</a> in north Wales called themselves the Jackdaw Society, after the jackdaws which live on the walls there. Formed in 1976, the society folded in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
