<p>The jackdaw is featured on the Ukrainian town of <a href="/wiki/Halych" title="Halych">Halych's</a> ancient coat of arms, the town's name allegedly being derived from the <a href="/wiki/East_Slavic_languages" title="East Slavic languages">East Slavic</a> word for the bird.<sup id="cite_ref-Magocsi_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magocsi-103"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> In his 1979 work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Laughter_and_Forgetting" title="The Book of Laughter and Forgetting">The Book of Laughter and Forgetting</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Milan_Kundera" title="Milan Kundera">Milan Kundera</a> notes that <a href="/wiki/Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka">Franz Kafka's</a> father Hermann had a sign in front of his shop with a jackdaw painted next to his name, since "kavka" means <i>jackdaw</i> in <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Ingoldsby_Legends" title="The Ingoldsby Legends">The Ingoldsby Legends</a></i> (1837) contains a poem by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Harris_Barham" title="Richard Harris Barham">Richard Harris Barham</a> named <i>The Jackdaw of <a href="/wiki/Rheims" title="Rheims" class="mw-redirect">Rheims</a></i>. At a banquet, while the cardinal is having his hands ceremoniously washed, his turquoise ring is stolen. Enraged, the cardinal puts a dreadful curse on the thief. To everybody's amazement, nobody present is affected by the curse, but a jackdaw flutters down, emaciated, bald, dishevelled and lame. It leads them to its nest in the belfry where the ring is discovered. The cardinal is delighted at the return of his ring and lifts the curse and, when some time later the jackdaw dies, canonises him, giving him the name "Jim Crow".<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Bourne" title="Vincent Bourne">Vincent Bourne</a>, a master of <a href="/wiki/Westminster_School" title="Westminster School">Westminster School</a>, wrote a Latin poem on the jackdaw which was translated into English by <a href="/wiki/William_Cowper" title="William Cowper">William Cowper</a> and forms the opening reference to a tale by <a href="/wiki/George_Augustus_Henry_Sala" title="George Augustus Henry Sala">George Augustus Henry Sala</a> about a royal wedding viewed from the point of view of a jackdaw.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=Western_Jackdaw&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Pest control">edit</a>]</span> <span class="mw-headline" id="Pest_control">Pest control</span></h3>
<p>The Western Jackdaw has been hunted as vermin, though not as heavily culled as other species of corvid.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> After a series of poor harvests in the early 1500s, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England"><span class="nowrap">Henry VIII</span></a> introduced a Vermin Act in 1532 "ordeyned to dystroye Choughes (i.e. Jackdaws), Crowes and Rokes" to protect grain crops from their predations. Western Jackdaws were notorious as they also favoured fruit, especially cherries. This act was taken up in a piecemeal fashion, but <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England"><span class="nowrap">Elizabeth I</span></a> passed the <i>Act for the Preservation of Grayne</i> in 1566 that was taken up with more vigour. The species was hunted for its threat to grain crops and for propensity for nesting in belfries until the mid-20th century. Particularly large numbers were culled in the county of <a href="/wiki/Norfolk" title="Norfolk">Norfolk</a>. Western Jackdaws were also culled on game estates as they raid nests of other birds for eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> In a 2003 dissertation on public opinion of corvids, Antonia Hereth notes that the German naturalist <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Brehm" title="Alfred Brehm">Alfred Brehm</a> considered the Western Jackdaw to be a lovable bird, and did not describe any negative impacts of this species on agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-hereth03_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hereth03-109"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Western Jackdaw is one of a very small number of birds that it is legal to use as a decoy or to trap in a cage in the United Kingdom. The other pest species that can be controlled by trapping are the Crow, Jay, Magpie and Rook. An authorised person must comply with the requirements of the <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_and_Countryside_Act_1981" title="Wildlife and Countryside Act 1981">Wildlife and Countryside Act 1981</a> and does not need to show that the birds were a nuisance before trapping them.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 2003 the Western Jackdaw was listed as a potential species for targeted hunting in the European Union <a href="/wiki/Birds_Directive" title="Birds Directive">Birds Directive</a>, and hunting has been encouraged by German hunting associations.<sup id="cite_ref-hereth03_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hereth03-109"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> Permission to shoot Western Jackdaws in spring and summer exists in Cyprus as they are thought (incorrectly) to prey on gamebirds.<sup id="cite_ref-Hadjisterkotis03_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hadjisterkotis03-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="citation book">Mayr, Ernst; Greenway, James C., Jr, ed.
